</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_body_weight" title="Template:Human body weight"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_body_weight" title="Template talk:Human body weight"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Human_body_weight&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Obesity</b> is a medical condition, sometimes considered a disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> in which abnormal or excess <a href="/wiki/Adipose_tissue" title="Adipose tissue">body fat</a> has accumulated to such an extent that it may have a negative effect on health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-11">[11]</a></sup> People are classified as obese when their <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> (BMI)—a measurement obtained by dividing a person's weight by the square of the person's height (despite known <a href="/wiki/Allometry" title="Allometry">allometric</a> inaccuracies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BogusAllometry_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-BogusAllometry-14">[a]</a></sup>)—is over <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002294199500000000♠"></span>30 <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kg</a>/<a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">m<sup>2</sup></a></span>; the range <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002245166250000000♠"></span>25–30 <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kg</a>/<a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">m<sup>2</sup></a></span> is defined as <a href="/wiki/Overweight" title="Overweight">overweight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> countries use lower values to calculate obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kan2005_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kan2005-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Obesity is a major cause of disability and is <a href="/wiki/Obesity-associated_morbidity" title="Obesity-associated morbidity">correlated with various diseases and conditions</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular diseases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_sleep_apnea" title="Obstructive sleep apnea">obstructive sleep apnea</a>, certain types of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[15]</a></sup> High BMI is a marker of risk for, but not a direct cause of, diseases caused by diet and physical activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[15]</a></sup> A reciprocal link has been found between obesity and <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>, with obesity increasing the risk of clinical depression, and also depression leading to a higher chance of developing obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luppino2010rev_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Luppino2010rev-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Obesity has individual, socioeconomic, and environmental causes. Some of the known causes are diet, physical activity, <a href="/wiki/Automation" title="Automation">automation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_trait_locus" title="Quantitative trait locus">genetic susceptibility</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medications" title="Medications">medications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mental_disorder" title="Mental disorder">mental disorders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_policy" title="Economic policy">economic policies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_disease" title="Endocrine disease">endocrine disorders</a>, and exposure to <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_disruptor" title="Endocrine disruptor">endocrine-disrupting chemicals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yaz2015_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yaz2015-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ble2008_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ble2008-18">[17]</a></sup>
Epidemiologic studies of overweight and obesity in children and adults covering 195 countries have shown that the prevalence of obesity has steadily increased in most countries, doubling in 73 countries between the years 1980 and 2015. As of 2015, the United States and China had the largest numbers of obese adults, and China and India had the largest numbers of obese children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NJM2017_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-NJM2017-7">[7]</a></sup> By 2018, 42% of Americans were obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bubnoff_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bubnoff-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>While a majority of obese individuals at any given time are attempting to lose weight and are often successful, research shows that maintaining that weight loss over the long term proves to be rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-20">[19]</a></sup> The reasons for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yo-yo_effect" title="Yo-yo effect">weight cycling</a> are not fully understood but may include decreased energy expenditure combined with an increased biological urge to eat during and after caloric restriction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-20">[19]</a></sup> More studies are needed to determine if weight cycling and yo-yo dieting contribute to inflammation and disease risk in obese individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although there is no effective, well-defined, evidence-based intervention for preventing obesity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[15]</a></sup> obesity prevention will require a complex approach, including interventions at community, family, and individual levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[15]</a></sup> Changes to <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">exercising</a> are the main treatments recommended by health professionals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup> Diet quality can be improved by reducing the consumption of energy-dense foods, such as those high in fat or sugars, and by increasing the intake of <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup> However, large-scale analyses have found an inverse relationship between energy density and energy cost of foods in developed nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anti-obesity_medication" title="Anti-obesity medication">Medications</a> can be used, along with a suitable diet, to reduce appetite or decrease fat absorption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yan2014_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yan2014-5">[5]</a></sup> If diet, exercise, and medication are not effective, a <a href="/wiki/Gastric_balloon" title="Gastric balloon">gastric balloon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bariatric_surgery" title="Bariatric surgery">surgery</a> may be performed to reduce stomach volume or length of the intestines, leading to feeling full earlier, or a reduced ability to absorb nutrients from food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Col2014_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Col2014-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-balloon2008_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-balloon2008-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Obesity is a leading <a href="/wiki/Preventable_causes_of_death" title="Preventable causes of death">preventable cause of death</a> worldwide, with increasing rates in adults and <a href="/wiki/Childhood_obesity" title="Childhood obesity">children</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> In 2015, 600 million adults (12%) and 100 million children were obese in 195 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NJM2017_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-NJM2017-7">[7]</a></sup> Obesity is more common in women than in men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015-1">[1]</a></sup> Public health officials view it as one of the most serious <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> problems of the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dibaise2013_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dibaise2013-24">[23]</a></sup> Today, obesity is <a href="/wiki/Social_stigma_of_obesity" title="Social stigma of obesity">stigmatized</a> in most of the world. Conversely, some cultures, past and present, have a favorable view of obesity, seeing it as a symbol of wealth and fertility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-25">[24]</a></sup> Nevertheless, in 2013, several medical societies, including the <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Heart_Association" title="American Heart Association">American Heart Association</a>, classified obesity as a <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGuidelines2013_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGuidelines2013-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes20130618_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes20130618-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_obesity" title="Classification of obesity">Classification of obesity</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; text-align:center">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Category<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</th>
<th>BMI (kg/m<sup>2</sup>)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">Underweight
</td>
<td>&lt; 18.5
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">Normal weight
</td>
<td><span class="nowrap">18.5 – 24.9</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">Overweight
</td>
<td>25.0 – 29.9
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">Obese (class I)
</td>
<td>30.0 – 34.9
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">Obese (class II)
</td>
<td>35.0 – 39.9
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">Obese (class III)
</td>
<td>≥ 40.0
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Obesity6.JPG"><img alt='A front and side view of a "super obese" male torso. Stretch marks of the skin are visible along with gynecomastia.' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1641" data-file-width="3428" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Obesity6.JPG/260px-Obesity6.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Obesity6.JPG/390px-Obesity6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Obesity6.JPG/520px-Obesity6.JPG 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Obesity6.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A "super obese" male with a BMI of 53 kg/m<sup>2</sup>: weight 182 kg (401 lb), height 185 cm (6 ft 1 in). He presents with <a href="/wiki/Stretch_marks" title="Stretch marks">stretch marks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adipomastia" title="Adipomastia">enlarged breasts</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Obesity is typically defined as a substantial accumulation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_fat" title="Body fat">body fat</a> that could impact health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOsheet_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOsheet-30">[29]</a></sup> Medical organizations tend to classify people as obese based on <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> (BMI) – a ratio of a person's weight in <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilograms</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Square_(algebra)" title="Square (algebra)">square</a> of their height in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">meters</a>. For adults, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) defines <i><a href="/wiki/Overweight" title="Overweight">overweight</a></i> as a BMI 25 or higher, and <i>obese</i> as a BMI 30 or higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOsheet_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHOsheet-30">[29]</a></sup> For children, obesity measures take age into consideration along with height and weight. For children aged 5–19, the WHO defines obesity as a BMI two <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviations</a> above the <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> for their age (a BMI around 18 for a five-year old; around 30 for a 19-year old).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOsheet_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHOsheet-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> For children under five, the WHO defines obesity as a weight three standard deviations above the median for their height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOsheet_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHOsheet-30">[29]</a></sup> The U.S. <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) further subdivides obesity based on BMI, with a BMI 30 to 35 called class 1 obesity; 35 to 40, class 2 obesity; and 40+, class 3 obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>It further evaluated in terms of fat distribution via the <a href="/wiki/Waist%E2%80%93hip_ratio" title="Waist–hip ratio">waist–hip ratio</a> and total cardiovascular risk factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweet2007_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet2007-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> BMI is closely related to both <a href="/wiki/Body_fat_percentage" title="Body fat percentage">percentage body fat</a> and total body fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In children, a healthy weight varies with age and sex. Obesity in children and adolescents is defined not as an absolute number but in relation to a historical normal group, such that obesity is a BMI greater than the 95th <a href="/wiki/Percentile" title="Percentile">percentile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc.gov_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-cdc.gov-36">[35]</a></sup> The reference data on which these percentiles were based date from 1963 to 1994, and thus have not been affected by the recent increases in weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flegal_KM,_Ogden_CL,_Wei_R,_Kuczmarski_RL,_Johnson_CL_2001_1086–93_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flegal_KM,_Ogden_CL,_Wei_R,_Kuczmarski_RL,_Johnson_CL_2001_1086–93-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some modifications to the WHO definitions have been made by particular organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bei2002_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bei2002-38">[37]</a></sup> The surgical literature breaks down class II and III or only class III obesity into further categories whose exact values are still disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morbid2007_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-morbid2007-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Any BMI ≥ 35 or 40 kg/m<sup>2</sup> is <i>severe obesity</i>.</li>
<li>A BMI of ≥ 35 kg/m<sup>2</sup> and experiencing obesity-related health conditions or ≥ 40 or 45 kg/m<sup>2</sup> is <i>morbid obesity</i>.</li>
<li>A BMI of ≥ 45 or 50 kg/m<sup>2</sup> is <i>super obesity</i>.</li></ul>
<p>As Asian populations develop negative health consequences at a lower BMI than <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">Caucasians</a>, some nations have redefined obesity; Japan has defined obesity as any BMI greater than 25 kg/m<sup>2</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kan2005_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kan2005-15">[14]</a></sup> while China uses a BMI of greater than 28 kg/m<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bei2002_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bei2002-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_health">Effects on health</span></h2>
<p>Excessive body <a href="/wiki/Human_body_weight" title="Human body weight">weight</a> has a strong link to many <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">diseases and conditions</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular diseases</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">diabetes mellitus type 2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_sleep_apnea" title="Obstructive sleep apnea">obstructive sleep apnea</a>, certain types of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poulain_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poulain-40">[39]</a></sup> As a result, obesity has been found to reduce <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mortality">Mortality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:350px"><div class="center noresize" style="width:350px;">
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1039153519">@media all and (max-width:720px){body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .mw-graph{min-width:100%!important;max-width:100%;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:visible}}.mw-parser-output .mw-graph-img{width:inherit;height:inherit}</style><div class="mw-graph mw-graph-always mw-graph-nofallback" data-graph-id="09b617c35b3096f10e828057f03cd624210a31f8" style="min-width:200px;min-height:150px"></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Relative risk of death over 10 years in white people who have never smoked in the United States by BMI. The BMI range 22.5 to 24.9 is set as the reference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Obesity is one of the leading <a href="/wiki/Preventable_causes_of_death" title="Preventable causes of death">preventable causes of death</a> worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barn1999_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barn1999-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allison_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allison-44">[43]</a></sup> A number of reviews have found that mortality risk is lowest at a BMI of 20–25 kg/m<sup>2</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2009_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2009-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2016Mort_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2016Mort-47">[46]</a></sup> in non-smokers and at 24–27 kg/m<sup>2</sup> in current smokers, with risk increasing along with changes in either direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Euro2008_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Euro2008-49">[48]</a></sup> This appears to apply in at least four continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2016Mort_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2016Mort-47">[46]</a></sup> Other evidence suggests that the association of BMI and waist circumference with mortality is U- or J-shaped, while the association between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waist-to-hip_ratio" title="Waist-to-hip ratio">waist-to-hip ratio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waist-to-height_ratio" title="Waist-to-height ratio">waist-to-height ratio</a> with mortality is more positive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> In Asians the risk of negative health effects begins to increase between 22 and 25 kg/m<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> In 2021, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> estimated that obesity caused at least 2.8 million deaths annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> On average, obesity reduces life expectancy by six to seven years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> a BMI of 30–35 kg/m<sup>2</sup> reduces life expectancy by two to four years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2009_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2009-46">[45]</a></sup> while severe obesity (BMI ≥ 40 kg/m<sup>2</sup>) reduces life expectancy by ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2009_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2009-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morbidity">Morbidity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Obesity-associated_morbidity" title="Obesity-associated morbidity">Obesity-associated morbidity</a></div>
<p>Obesity increases the risk of many physical and mental conditions. These comorbidities are most commonly shown in <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_syndrome" title="Metabolic syndrome">metabolic syndrome</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup> a combination of medical disorders which includes: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">diabetes mellitus type 2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">high blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypercholesterolemia" title="Hypercholesterolemia">high blood cholesterol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hypertriglyceridemia" title="Hypertriglyceridemia">high triglyceride levels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> A study from the <a href="/wiki/RAK_Hospital" title="RAK Hospital">RAK Hospital</a> found that obese people are at a greater risk of developing <a href="/wiki/Long_COVID" title="Long COVID">long COVID</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> The CDC has found that obesity is the single strongest risk factor for severe COVID-19 illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Complications are either directly caused by obesity or indirectly related through mechanisms sharing a common cause such as a poor diet or a <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">sedentary lifestyle</a>. The strength of the link between obesity and specific conditions varies. One of the strongest is the link with <a href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a>. Excess body fat underlies 64% of cases of diabetes in men and 77% of cases in women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Health consequences fall into two broad categories: those attributable to the effects of increased fat mass (such as <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_sleep_apnea" title="Obstructive sleep apnea">obstructive sleep apnea</a>, social stigmatization) and those due to the increased number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fat_cells" title="Fat cells">fat cells</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-alcoholic_fatty_liver_disease" title="Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease">non-alcoholic fatty liver disease</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bray2004_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bray2004-58">[57]</a></sup> Increases in body fat alter the body's response to insulin, potentially leading to <a href="/wiki/Insulin_resistance" title="Insulin resistance">insulin resistance</a>. Increased fat also creates a <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">proinflammatory state</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Thrombosis" title="Thrombosis">prothrombotic</a> state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bray2004_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bray2004-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Ambox current red Americas.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">Parts of this article (those related to table below) need to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2022</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metrics_of_health">Metrics of health</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metabolically_healthy_obesity" title="Metabolically healthy obesity">Metabolically healthy obesity</a></div>
<p>Newer research has focused on methods of identifying healthier obese people by clinicians, and not treating obese people as a monolithic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Obese people who do not experience medical complications from their obesity are sometimes called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolically_healthy_obese" title="Metabolically healthy obese">(metabolically) healthy obese</a></i>, but the extent to which this group exists (especially among older people) is in dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-85">[84]</a></sup> The number of people considered <i>metabolically healthy</i> depends on the definition used, and there is no universally accepted definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-86">[85]</a></sup> There are numerous obese people who have relatively few metabolic abnormalities, and a minority of obese people have no medical complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-86">[85]</a></sup> The guidelines of the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_of_Clinical_Endocrinologists" title="American Association of Clinical Endocrinologists">American Association of Clinical Endocrinologists</a> call for physicians to use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Risk_stratification" title="Risk stratification">risk stratification</a> with obese patients when considering how to assess their risk of developing type 2 diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 59–60">: 59–60 </span></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, the BioSHaRE–<a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> Healthy Obese Project (sponsored by Maelstrom Research, a team under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Research_Institute_of_the_McGill_University_Health_Centre" title="Research Institute of the McGill University Health Centre">Research Institute of the McGill University Health Centre</a>) came up with two definitions for <i>healthy obesity</i>, one more strict and one less so:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="background-color:rgba(0,0,0,0);border:none">
<caption>BioSHaRE Healthy Obese (HOP) Project Criteria (2014)<br/><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047488">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold">A patient must have a <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> ≥ 30, and all of the following:</span>
</caption>

<td colspan="2">≤ 2.1

</td></tr>

<th colspan="3">No diagnosis of any <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>
</th></tr></tbody></table>
<p>To come up with these criteria, BioSHaRE controlled for age and tobacco use, researching how both may effect the metabolic syndrome associated with obesity, but not found to exist in the metabolically healthy obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Other definitions of metabolically healthy obesity exist, including ones based on waist circumference rather than BMI, which is unreliable in certain individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another identification metric for health in obese people is <a href="/wiki/Triceps_surae_muscle" title="Triceps surae muscle">calf</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_strength" title="Muscle strength">strength</a>, which is positively correlated with <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">physical fitness</a> in obese people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Body_composition" title="Body composition">Body composition</a> in general is hypothesized to help explain the existence of metabolically healthy obesity—the metabolically healthy obese are often found to have low amounts of <a href="/wiki/Ectopia_(medicine)" title="Ectopia (medicine)">ectopic</a> fat (fat stored in tissues other than adipose tissue) despite having overall fat mass equivalent in weight to obese people with <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_syndrome" title="Metabolic syndrome">metabolic syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 1282">: 1282 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Survival_paradox">Survival paradox</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Obesity_paradox" title="Obesity paradox">Obesity paradox</a></div>
<p>Although the negative health consequences of obesity in the general population are well supported by the available evidence, health outcomes in certain subgroups seem to be improved at an increased BMI, a phenomenon known as the obesity survival paradox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2007_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2007-92">[91]</a></sup> The paradox was first described in 1999 in overweight and obese people undergoing <a href="/wiki/Hemodialysis" title="Hemodialysis">hemodialysis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt2007_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt2007-92">[91]</a></sup> and has subsequently been found in those with <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peripheral_vascular_disease" title="Peripheral vascular disease">peripheral artery disease</a> (PAD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paradox2003_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-paradox2003-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In people with heart failure, those with a BMI between 30.0 and 34.9 had lower mortality than those with a normal weight. This has been attributed to the fact that people often lose weight as they become progressively more ill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> Similar findings have been made in other types of heart disease. People with class I obesity and heart disease do not have greater rates of further heart problems than people of normal weight who also have heart disease. In people with greater degrees of obesity, however, the risk of further cardiovascular events is increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> Even after <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_bypass_surgery" title="Coronary artery bypass surgery">cardiac bypass surgery</a>, no increase in mortality is seen in the overweight and obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> One study found that the improved survival could be explained by the more aggressive treatment obese people receive after a cardiac event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> Another study found that if one takes into account <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a> (COPD) in those with PAD, the benefit of obesity no longer exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paradox2003_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-paradox2003-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>
<p>The "<a href="/wiki/A_calorie_is_a_calorie" title="A calorie is a calorie">a calorie is a calorie</a>" model of obesity posits a combination of excessive <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a> intake and a lack of <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a> as the cause of most cases of obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CADG2006_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-99">[98]</a></sup> A limited number of cases are due primarily to genetics, medical reasons, or psychiatric illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ble2008_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ble2008-18">[17]</a></sup> In contrast, increasing rates of obesity at a societal level are felt to be due to an easily accessible and palatable diet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> increased <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_the_car_on_societies" title="Effects of the car on societies">reliance on cars</a>, and mechanized manufacturing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James2008_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-James2008-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2006 review identified ten other possible contributors to the recent increase of obesity: (1) <a href="/wiki/Sleep_debt" title="Sleep debt">insufficient sleep</a>, (2) <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_disruptor" title="Endocrine disruptor">endocrine disruptors</a> (environmental <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a> that interfere with lipid metabolism), (3) decreased variability in ambient temperature, (4) decreased rates of <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">smoking</a>, because smoking suppresses appetite, (5) increased use of medications that can cause weight gain (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atypical_antipsychotics" title="Atypical antipsychotics">atypical antipsychotics</a>), (6) proportional increases in ethnic and age groups that tend to be heavier, (7) pregnancy at a later age (which may cause susceptibility to obesity in children), (8) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic" title="Epigenetic">epigenetic</a> risk factors passed on generationally, (9) <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> for higher BMI, and (10) <a href="/wiki/Assortative_mating" title="Assortative mating">assortative mating</a> leading to increased concentration of obesity risk factors (this would increase the number of obese people by increasing population variance in weight).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16801930_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16801930-103">[102]</a></sup> According to the Endocrine Society, there is "growing evidence suggesting that obesity is a disorder of the <a href="/wiki/Energy_homeostasis" title="Energy homeostasis">energy homeostasis</a> system, rather than simply arising from the passive accumulation of excess weight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diet_and_obesity" title="Diet and obesity">Diet and obesity</a></div>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png"><img alt="A graph showing a gradual increase in global food energy consumption per person per day between 1961 and 2002." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="467" data-file-width="817" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png/350px-World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png/525px-World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png/700px-World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Per_Person_Energy_Consumption.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Average per capita energy consumption of the world from 1961 to 2002<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earth09_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-105">[104]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A 2016 review supported excess appetite for palatable, high-calorie food (especially fat, sugar, and certain animal proteins) as the primary factor driving obesity worldwide, likely because of imbalances in neurotransmitters affecting the drive to eat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pharmaReview_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-pharmaReview-106">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dietary_energy_supply" title="Dietary energy supply">Dietary energy supply</a> per capita varies markedly between different regions and countries. It has also changed significantly over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earth09_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-105">[104]</a></sup> From the early 1970s to the late 1990s the average food energy available per person per day (the amount of food bought) increased in all parts of the world except Eastern Europe. The United States had the highest availability with 3,654 calories (15,290 kJ) per person in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earth09_105-3"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-105">[104]</a></sup> This increased further in 2003 to 3,754 calories (15,710 kJ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earth09_105-4"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-105">[104]</a></sup> During the late 1990s, Europeans had 3,394 calories (14,200 kJ) per person, in the developing areas of Asia there were 2,648 calories (11,080 kJ) per person, and in sub-Saharan Africa people had 2,176 calories (9,100 kJ) per person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earth09_105-5"><a href="#cite_note-Earth09-105">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> Total food energy consumption has been found to be related to obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The widespread availability of <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Guidelines_for_Americans" title="Dietary Guidelines for Americans">dietary guidelines</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> has done little to address the problems of overeating and poor dietary choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> From 1971 to 2000, obesity rates in the United States increased from 14.5% to 30.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flegal2002_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flegal2002-111">[110]</a></sup> During the same period, an increase occurred in the average amount of food energy consumed. For women, the average increase was 335 calories (1,400 kJ) per day (1,542 calories (6,450 kJ) in 1971 and 1,877 calories (7,850 kJ) in 2004), while for men the average increase was 168 calories (700 kJ) per day (2,450 calories (10,300 kJ) in 1971 and 2,618 calories (10,950 kJ) in 2004). Most of this extra food energy came from an increase in carbohydrate consumption rather than fat consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> The primary sources of these extra carbohydrates are sweetened beverages, which now account for almost 25 percent of daily food energy in young adults in America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caballero_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-113">[112]</a></sup> and potato chips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> Consumption of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweetened_beverages" title="Sweetened beverages">sweetened beverages</a> such as soft drinks, fruit drinks, and iced tea is believed to be contributing to the rising rates of obesity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> and to an increased risk of metabolic syndrome and type 2 diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D_deficiency" title="Vitamin D deficiency">Vitamin D deficiency</a> is related to diseases associated with obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WambergPedersen2015_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-WambergPedersen2015-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>As societies become increasingly reliant on <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">energy-dense</a>, big-portions, and fast-food meals, the association between fast-food consumption and obesity becomes more concerning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> In the United States, consumption of fast-food meals tripled and food energy intake from these meals quadrupled between 1977 and 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Agricultural_policy" title="Agricultural policy">Agricultural policy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_Revolution_(agriculture)" title="Green Revolution (agriculture)">techniques</a> in the United States and Europe have led to lower <a href="/wiki/Food_prices" title="Food prices">food prices</a>. In the United States, subsidization of corn, soy, wheat, and rice through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._farm_bill" title="U.S. farm bill">U.S. farm bill</a> has made the main sources of processed food cheap compared to fruits and vegetables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calorie_count_laws" title="Calorie count laws">Calorie count laws</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nutrition_facts_label" title="Nutrition facts label">nutrition facts labels</a> attempt to steer people toward making healthier food choices, including awareness of how much food energy is being consumed.
</p><p>Obese people consistently under-report their food consumption as compared to people of normal weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> This is supported both by tests of people carried out in a <a href="/wiki/Calorimeter" title="Calorimeter">calorimeter</a> room<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> and by direct observation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sedentary_lifestyle">Sedentary lifestyle</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">Sedentary lifestyle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exercise_trends" title="Exercise trends">Exercise trends</a></div>
<p>A sedentary lifestyle plays a significant role in obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> Worldwide there has been a large shift towards less physically demanding work,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2009_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOExercise_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOExercise-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness2006_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ness2006-127">[126]</a></sup> and currently at least 30% of the world's population gets insufficient exercise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOExercise_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHOExercise-126">[125]</a></sup> This is primarily due to increasing use of mechanized transportation and a greater prevalence of labor-saving technology in the home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2009_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOExercise_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHOExercise-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness2006_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ness2006-127">[126]</a></sup> In children, there appear to be declines in levels of physical activity (with particularly strong declines in the amount of walking and physical education), likely due to safety concerns, changes in social interaction (such as fewer relationships with neighborhood children), and inadequate urban design (such as too few public spaces for safe physical activity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatric_Fitness_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatric_Fitness-128">[127]</a></sup> World trends in active leisure time <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a> are less clear. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> indicates people worldwide are taking up less active recreational pursuits, while a study from Finland<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> found an increase and a study from the United States found leisure-time physical activity has not changed significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> A 2011 review of physical activity in children found that it may not be a significant contributor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wil2011_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wil2011-131">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>In both children and adults, there is an association between television viewing time and the risk of obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> A review found 63 of 73 studies (86%) showed an increased rate of childhood obesity with increased media exposure, with rates increasing proportionally to time spent watching television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetics_of_obesity" title="Genetics of obesity">Genetics of obesity</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Ambox current red Americas.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2021</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_monstrua_desnuda_(1680),_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg"><img alt="A painting of a dark haired pink cheeked obese nude young female leaning against a table. She is holding grapes and grape leaves in her left hand which cover her genitalia." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3051" data-file-width="2049" decoding="async" height="432" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg/290px-La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg/435px-La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg/580px-La_monstrua_desnuda_%281680%29%2C_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_monstrua_desnuda_(1680),_de_Juan_Carre%C3%B1o_de_Miranda..jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1680 painting by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juan_Carreno_de_Miranda" title="Juan Carreno de Miranda">Juan Carreno de Miranda</a> of a girl presumed to have <a href="/wiki/Prader%E2%80%93Willi_syndrome" title="Prader–Willi syndrome">Prader–Willi syndrome</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Like many other medical conditions, obesity is the result of an interplay between genetic and environmental factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">Polymorphisms</a> in various <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> controlling <a href="/wiki/Appetite" title="Appetite">appetite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> predispose to obesity when sufficient food energy is present. As of 2006, more than 41 of these sites on the human genome have been linked to the development of obesity when a favorable environment is present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> People with two copies of the <a href="/wiki/FTO_gene" title="FTO gene">FTO gene</a> (fat mass and obesity associated gene) have been found on average to weigh 3–4 kg more and have a 1.67-fold greater risk of obesity compared with those without the risk <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">allele</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> The differences in BMI between people that are <a href="/wiki/Heritability" title="Heritability">due to genetics</a> varies depending on the population examined from 6% to 85%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Obesity is a major feature in several syndromes, such as <a href="/wiki/Prader%E2%80%93Willi_syndrome" title="Prader–Willi syndrome">Prader–Willi syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bardet%E2%80%93Biedl_syndrome" title="Bardet–Biedl syndrome">Bardet–Biedl syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cohen_syndrome" title="Cohen syndrome">Cohen syndrome</a>, and <a href="/wiki/MOMO_syndrome" title="MOMO syndrome">MOMO syndrome</a>. (The term "non-syndromic obesity" is sometimes used to exclude these conditions.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19506576_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19506576-141">[140]</a></sup> In people with early-onset severe obesity (defined by an onset before 10 years of age and body mass index over three <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviations</a> above normal), 7% harbor a single point DNA mutation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies that have focused on inheritance patterns rather than on specific genes have found that 80% of the offspring of two <a href="/wiki/Parental_obesity" title="Parental obesity">obese parents</a> were also obese, in contrast to less than 10% of the offspring of two parents who were of normal weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> Different people exposed to the same environment have different risks of obesity due to their underlying genetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Thrifty_gene_hypothesis" title="Thrifty gene hypothesis">thrifty gene hypothesis</a> postulates that, due to dietary scarcity during human evolution, people are prone to obesity. Their ability to take advantage of rare periods of abundance by storing energy as fat would be advantageous during times of varying food availability, and individuals with greater adipose reserves would be more likely to survive <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>. This tendency to store fat, however, would be maladaptive in societies with stable food supplies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (July 2021)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This theory has received various criticisms, and other evolutionarily-based theories such as the <a href="/wiki/Drifty_gene_hypothesis" title="Drifty gene hypothesis">drifty gene hypothesis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Thrifty_phenotype" title="Thrifty phenotype">thrifty phenotype hypothesis</a> have also been proposed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (July 2021)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_illnesses">Other illnesses</span></h3>
<p>Certain physical and mental illnesses and the pharmaceutical substances used to treat them can increase risk of obesity. Medical illnesses that increase obesity risk include several rare genetic syndromes (listed above) as well as some congenital or acquired conditions: <a href="/wiki/Hypothyroidism" title="Hypothyroidism">hypothyroidism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cushing%27s_syndrome" title="Cushing's syndrome">Cushing's syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone_deficiency" title="Growth hormone deficiency">growth hormone deficiency</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> and some <a href="/wiki/Eating_disorder" title="Eating disorder">eating disorders</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Binge_eating_disorder" title="Binge eating disorder">binge eating disorder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Night_eating_syndrome" title="Night eating syndrome">night eating syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-28"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup> However, obesity is not regarded as a psychiatric disorder, and therefore is not listed in the <a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">DSM-IVR</a> as a psychiatric illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> The risk of overweight and obesity is higher in patients with psychiatric disorders than in persons without psychiatric disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Certain medications may cause weight gain or changes in <a href="/wiki/Body_composition" title="Body composition">body composition</a>; these include <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfonylurea" title="Sulfonylurea">sulfonylureas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thiazolidinedione" title="Thiazolidinedione">thiazolidinediones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atypical_antipsychotic" title="Atypical antipsychotic">atypical antipsychotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antidepressant" title="Antidepressant">antidepressants</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucocorticoids" title="Glucocorticoids">steroids</a>, certain <a href="/wiki/Anticonvulsant" title="Anticonvulsant">anticonvulsants</a> (<a href="/wiki/Phenytoin" title="Phenytoin">phenytoin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valproate" title="Valproate">valproate</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pizotifen" title="Pizotifen">pizotifen</a>, and some forms of <a href="/wiki/Hormonal_contraception" title="Hormonal contraception">hormonal contraception</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-29"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_determinants">Social determinants</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_determinants_of_obesity" title="Social determinants of obesity">Social determinants of obesity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="378" decoding="async" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG/290px-Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG 1.5x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yamai_no_Soshi_-_Obesity.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The disease scroll (<i>Yamai no soshi</i>, late 12th century) depicts a woman moneylender with obesity, considered a disease of the rich.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:More_adults_are_obese_in_more_unequal_rich_countries_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="753" data-file-width="1054" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/More_adults_are_obese_in_more_unequal_rich_countries_%28cropped%29.jpg/290px-More_adults_are_obese_in_more_unequal_rich_countries_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/More_adults_are_obese_in_more_unequal_rich_countries_%28cropped%29.jpg/435px-More_adults_are_obese_in_more_unequal_rich_countries_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/More_adults_are_obese_in_more_unequal_rich_countries_%28cropped%29.jpg/580px-More_adults_are_obese_in_more_unequal_rich_countries_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:More_adults_are_obese_in_more_unequal_rich_countries_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Obesity in developed countries is correlated with <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>While genetic influences are important to understanding obesity, they cannot explain the current<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (July 2021)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> dramatic increase seen within specific countries or globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2021)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Though it is accepted that energy consumption in excess of energy expenditure leads to increases in body weight on an individual basis, the cause of the shifts in these two factors on the societal scale is much debated. There are a number of theories as to the cause but most believe it is a combination of various factors.
</p><p>The correlation between <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social class</a> and BMI varies globally. A review in 1989 found that in developed countries women of a high social class were less likely to be obese. No significant differences were seen among men of different social classes. In the developing world, women, men, and children from high social classes had greater rates of obesity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2021)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> An update of this review carried out in 2007 found the same relationships, but they were weaker. The decrease in strength of correlation was felt to be due to the effects of <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLaren2007_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLaren2007-150">[149]</a></sup> Among developed countries, levels of adult obesity, and percentage of teenage children who are overweight, are correlated with <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">income inequality</a>. A similar relationship is seen among US states: more adults, even in higher social classes, are obese in more unequal states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spirit_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-spirit-151">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many explanations have been put forth for associations between BMI and social class. It is thought that in developed countries, the wealthy are able to afford more nutritious food, they are under greater social pressure to remain slim, and have more opportunities along with greater expectations for <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">physical fitness</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Undeveloped_countries" title="Undeveloped countries">undeveloped countries</a> the ability to afford food, high energy expenditure with physical labor, and cultural values favoring a larger body size are believed to contribute to the observed patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLaren2007_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-McLaren2007-150">[149]</a></sup> Attitudes toward body weight held by people in one's life may also play a role in obesity. A correlation in BMI changes over time has been found among friends, siblings, and spouses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup> Stress and perceived low social status appear to increase risk of obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spirit_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-spirit-151">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Smoking has a significant effect on an individual's weight. Those who quit smoking gain an average of 4.4 kilograms (9.7 lb) for men and 5.0 kilograms (11.0 lb) for women over ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> However, changing rates of smoking have had little effect on the overall rates of obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the number of children a person has is related to their risk of obesity. A woman's risk increases by 7% per child, while a man's risk increases by 4% per child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> This could be partly explained by the fact that having dependent children decreases physical activity in Western parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the developing world urbanization is playing a role in increasing rate of obesity. In China overall rates of obesity are below 5%; however, in some cities rates of obesity are greater than 20%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> In part, this may be because of urban design issues (such as inadequate public spaces for physical activity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatric_Fitness_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatric_Fitness-128">[127]</a></sup> Time spent in motor vehicles, as opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_transportation" title="Active transportation">active transportation</a> options such as cycling or walking, is correlated with increased risk of obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">Malnutrition</a> in early life is believed to play a role in the rising rates of obesity in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DC2001_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-DC2001-162">[161]</a></sup> Endocrine changes that occur during periods of malnutrition may promote the storage of fat once more food energy becomes available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DC2001_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-DC2001-162">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gut_bacteria">Gut bacteria</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Infectobesity" title="Infectobesity">Infectobesity</a></div>
<p>The study of the effect of infectious agents on metabolism is still in its early stages. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">Gut flora</a> has been shown to differ between lean and obese people. There is an indication that gut flora can affect the metabolic potential. This apparent alteration is believed to confer a greater capacity to harvest energy contributing to obesity. Whether these differences are the direct cause or the result of obesity has yet to be determined unequivocally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> The use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">antibiotics</a> among children has also been associated with obesity later in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cox2015_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2015-165">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>An association between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses">viruses</a> and obesity has been found in humans and several different animal species. The amount that these associations may have contributed to the rising rate of obesity is yet to be determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_factors">Other factors</span></h3>
<p>A number of reviews have found an association between short duration of sleep and obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cap2008_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cap2008-167">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup> Whether one causes the other is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cap2008_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cap2008-167">[166]</a></sup> Even if short sleep does increase weight gain it is unclear if this is to a meaningful degree or if increasing sleep would be of benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>Certain aspects of <a href="/wiki/Personality" title="Personality">personality</a> are associated with being obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ger2014_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ger2014-170">[169]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Loneliness" title="Loneliness">Loneliness</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neuroticism" title="Neuroticism">neuroticism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Impulsivity" title="Impulsivity">impulsivity</a>, and sensitivity to reward are more common in people who are obese while <a href="/wiki/Conscientiousness" title="Conscientiousness">conscientiousness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Self-control" title="Self-control">self-control</a> are less common in people who are obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ger2014_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ger2014-170">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup> Because most of the studies on this topic are questionnaire-based, it is possible that these findings overestimate the relationships between personality and obesity: people who are obese might be aware of the <a href="/wiki/Social_stigma_of_obesity" title="Social stigma of obesity">social stigma of obesity</a> and their questionnaire responses might be biased accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ger2014_170-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ger2014-170">[169]</a></sup> Similarly, the personalities of people who are obese as children might be influenced by obesity stigma, rather than these personality factors acting as risk factors for obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ger2014_170-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ger2014-170">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiology_of_obesity" title="Pathophysiology of obesity">Pathophysiology of obesity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fatmouse.jpg"><img alt="Two white mice both with similar sized ears, black eyes, and pink noses. The body of the mouse on the left, however, is about three times the width of the normal sized mouse on the right." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Fatmouse.jpg/290px-Fatmouse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Fatmouse.jpg/435px-Fatmouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Fatmouse.jpg/580px-Fatmouse.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fatmouse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A comparison of a mouse unable to produce <a href="/wiki/Leptin" title="Leptin">leptin</a> thus resulting in obesity (left) and a normal mouse (right)</div></div></div>
<p>Two distinct but related processes are considered to be involved in the development of obesity: sustained positive energy balance (energy intake exceeding energy expenditure) and the resetting of the body weight "set point" at an increased value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28898979_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28898979-173">[172]</a></sup> The second process explains why finding effective obesity treatments has been difficult. While the underlying biology of this process still remains uncertain, research is beginning to clarify the mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28898979_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28898979-173">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>At a biological level, there are many possible <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiology" title="Pathophysiology">pathophysiological</a> mechanisms involved in the development and maintenance of obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flier_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-flier-174">[173]</a></sup> This field of research had been almost unapproached until the <a href="/wiki/Leptin" title="Leptin">leptin</a> gene was discovered in 1994 by J. M. Friedman's laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup> While leptin and <a href="/wiki/Ghrelin" title="Ghrelin">ghrelin</a> are produced peripherally, they control appetite through their actions on the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>. In particular, they and other appetite-related hormones act on the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a>, a region of the brain central to the regulation of food intake and energy expenditure. There are several circuits within the hypothalamus that contribute to its role in integrating appetite, the <a href="/wiki/Melanocortin" title="Melanocortin">melanocortin</a> pathway being the most well understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flier_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-flier-174">[173]</a></sup> The circuit begins with an area of the hypothalamus, the <a href="/wiki/Arcuate_nucleus" title="Arcuate nucleus">arcuate nucleus</a>, that has outputs to the <a href="/wiki/Lateral_hypothalamus" title="Lateral hypothalamus">lateral hypothalamus</a> (LH) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventromedial_hypothalamus" title="Ventromedial hypothalamus">ventromedial hypothalamus</a> (VMH), the brain's feeding and satiety centers, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The arcuate nucleus contains two distinct groups of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flier_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-flier-174">[173]</a></sup> The first group coexpresses <a href="/wiki/Neuropeptide_Y" title="Neuropeptide Y">neuropeptide Y</a> (NPY) and <a href="/wiki/Agouti-related_peptide" title="Agouti-related peptide">agouti-related peptide</a> (AgRP) and has stimulatory inputs to the LH and inhibitory inputs to the VMH. The second group coexpresses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pro-opiomelanocortin" title="Pro-opiomelanocortin">pro-opiomelanocortin</a> (POMC) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cocaine-_and_amphetamine-regulated_transcript" title="Cocaine- and amphetamine-regulated transcript">cocaine- and amphetamine-regulated transcript</a> (CART) and has stimulatory inputs to the VMH and inhibitory inputs to the LH. Consequently, NPY/AgRP neurons stimulate feeding and inhibit satiety, while POMC/CART neurons stimulate satiety and inhibit feeding. Both groups of arcuate nucleus neurons are regulated in part by leptin. Leptin inhibits the NPY/AgRP group while stimulating the POMC/CART group. Thus a deficiency in leptin signaling, either via leptin deficiency or leptin resistance, leads to overfeeding and may account for some genetic and acquired forms of obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flier_174-3"><a href="#cite_note-flier-174">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_health">Public health</span></h2>
