<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) predicts that <a href="/wiki/Overweight" title="Overweight">overweight</a> and obesity may soon<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (July 2021)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> replace more traditional <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> concerns such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Undernutrition" title="Undernutrition">undernutrition</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_diseases" title="Infectious diseases">infectious diseases</a> as the most significant cause of poor health.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2021)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> Obesity is a public health and policy problem because of its prevalence, costs, and health effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Preventive_Services_Task_Force" title="United States Preventive Services Task Force">United States Preventive Services Task Force</a> recommends screening for all adults followed by behavioral interventions in those who are obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup> Public health efforts seek to understand and correct the <a href="/wiki/Obesity_and_the_environment" title="Obesity and the environment">environmental factors</a> responsible for the increasing prevalence of obesity in the population. Solutions look at changing the factors that cause excess food energy consumption and inhibit physical activity. Efforts include federally reimbursed meal programs in schools, limiting direct <a href="/wiki/Junk_food" title="Junk food">junk</a> <a href="/wiki/Food_marketing" title="Food marketing">food marketing</a> to children,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup> and decreasing access to sugar-sweetened beverages in schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> The World Health Organization recommends the taxing of sugary drinks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup> When constructing urban environments, efforts have been made to increase access to parks and to develop pedestrian routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup> There is low quality evidence that nutritional labelling with energy information on menus can help to reduce energy intake while dining in restaurants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reports">Reports</span></h3>
<p>Many organizations have published reports pertaining to obesity. In 1998, the first US Federal guidelines were published, titled "Clinical Guidelines on the Identification, Evaluation, and Treatment of Overweight and Obesity in Adults: The Evidence Report".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup> In 2006, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Obesity_Network" title="Canadian Obesity Network">Canadian Obesity Network</a>, now known as <a href="/wiki/Obesity_Canada" title="Obesity Canada">Obesity Canada</a> published the "Canadian Clinical Practice Guidelines (CPG) on the Management and Prevention of Obesity in Adults and Children". This is a comprehensive evidence-based guideline to address the management and prevention of overweight and obesity in adults and children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CADG2006_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, the United Kingdom <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Physicians" title="Royal College of Physicians">Royal College of Physicians</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Faculty_of_Public_Health" title="Faculty of Public Health">Faculty of Public Health</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Paediatrics_and_Child_Health" title="Royal College of Paediatrics and Child Health">Royal College of Paediatrics and Child Health</a> released the report "Storing up Problems", which highlighted the growing problem of obesity in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup> The same year, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Health_Select_Committee" title="Health Select Committee">Health Select Committee</a> published its "most comprehensive inquiry [...] ever undertaken" into the impact of obesity on health and society in the UK and possible approaches to the problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GB2004_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-GB2004-187">[186]</a></sup> In 2006, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Health_and_Clinical_Excellence" title="National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence">National Institute for Health and Clinical Excellence</a> (NICE) issued a guideline on the diagnosis and management of obesity, as well as policy implications for non-healthcare organizations such as local councils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup> A 2007 report produced by <a href="/wiki/Derek_Wanless" title="Derek Wanless">Derek Wanless</a> for the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Fund" title="King's Fund">King's Fund</a> warned that unless further action was taken, obesity had the capacity to debilitate the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> financially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> In 2022 the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Health_and_Care_Research" title="National Institute for Health and Care Research">National Institute for Health and Care Research</a> (NIHR) published a comprehensive review of research on what <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_England" title="Local government in England">local authorities</a> can do to reduce obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Obesity Policy Action (OPA) framework divides measure into <i>upstream</i> policies, <i>midstream</i> policies, and <i>downstream</i> policies. Upstream policies have to do with changing society, while midstream policies try to alter behaviors believed to contribute to obesity at the individual level, while downstream policies treat currently obese people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Management_of_obesity" title="Management of obesity">Management of obesity</a></div>
<p>The main treatment for obesity consists of <a href="/wiki/Weight_loss" title="Weight loss">weight loss</a> via lifestyle interventions, including prescribed <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diets</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">physical exercise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGuidelines2013_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGuidelines2013-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CADG2006_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDietaryGuidelines2015_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDietaryGuidelines2015-192">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AACAHA2019_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-AACAHA2019-193">[192]</a></sup> Although it is unclear what diets might support long-term weight loss, and although the effectiveness of low-calorie diets is debated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strychar_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strychar-194">[193]</a></sup> lifestyle changes that reduce calorie consumption or increase physical exercise over the long term also tend to produce some sustained weight loss, despite slow weight regain over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGuidelines2013_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGuidelines2013-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strychar_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strychar-194">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup> Although 87% of participants in the <a href="/wiki/National_Weight_Control_Registry" title="National Weight Control Registry">National Weight Control Registry</a> were able to maintain 10% body weight loss for 10 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="unclear if this is sufficient weight loss to reduce BMI below the obesity cutoff and therefore effectively manage obesity (February 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the most appropriate dietary approach for long term weight loss maintenance is still unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yannakoulia_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yannakoulia-198">[197]</a></sup> In the US, intensive behavioral interventions combining both dietary changes and exercise are recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGuidelines2013_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-USGuidelines2013-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDietaryGuidelines2015_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-USDietaryGuidelines2015-192">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup> Intermittent fasting has no additional benefit of weight loss compared to continuous energy restriction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yannakoulia_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yannakoulia-198">[197]</a></sup> Adherence is a more important factor in weight loss success than whatever kind of diet an individual undertakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yannakoulia_198-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yannakoulia-198">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several hypo-caloric diets are effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGuidelines2013_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-USGuidelines2013-26">[25]</a></sup> In the short-term low carbohydrate diets appear better than low fat diets for weight loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBU2013_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2013-201">[200]</a></sup> In the long term, however, all types of low-carbohydrate and low-fat diets appear equally beneficial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBU2013_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2013-201">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup> A 2014 review found that the heart disease and diabetes risks associated with different diets appear to be similar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup> Promotion of the Mediterranean diets among the obese may lower the risk of heart disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBU2013_201-2"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2013-201">[200]</a></sup> Decreased intake of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweet_drink" title="Sweet drink">sweet drinks</a> is also related to weight-loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBU2013_201-3"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2013-201">[200]</a></sup> Success rates of long-term weight loss maintenance with lifestyle changes are low, ranging from 2–20%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup> Dietary and lifestyle changes are effective in limiting excessive weight gain in <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> and improve outcomes for both the mother and the child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup> Intensive behavioral counseling is recommended in those who are both obese and have other risk factors for heart disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_interventions">Medical interventions</span></h2>
<p>Five medications have evidence for long-term use <a href="/wiki/Orlistat" title="Orlistat">orlistat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lorcaserin" title="Lorcaserin">lorcaserin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liraglutide" title="Liraglutide">liraglutide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phentermine/topiramate" title="Phentermine/topiramate">phentermine–topiramate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Naltrexone/bupropion" title="Naltrexone/bupropion">naltrexone–bupropion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM_2017_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM_2017-207">[206]</a></sup> They result in weight loss after one year ranged from 3.0 to 6.7 kg (6.6-14.8 lbs) over placebo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM_2017_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM_2017-207">[206]</a></sup> Orlistat, liraglutide, and naltrexone–bupropion are available in both the United States and Europe, phentermine–topiramate is available only in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMA2013_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMA2013-208">[207]</a></sup> European regulatory authorities rejected lorcaserin and phentermine-topiramate, in part because of associations of heart valve problems with lorcaserin and more general heart and blood vessel problems with phentermine–topiramate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMA2013_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-EMA2013-208">[207]</a></sup> Lorcaserin was available in the United States and then removed from the market in 2020 due to its association with cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup> Orlistat use is associated with high rates of gastrointestinal side effects<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orli07_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orli07-210">[209]</a></sup> and concerns have been raised about negative effects on the kidneys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup> There is no information on how these drugs affect longer-term complications of obesity such as cardiovascular disease or death,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yan2014_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yan2014-5">[5]</a></sup> however, liraglutide, when used for type 2 diabetes, does reduce cardiovascular events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most effective treatment for obesity is <a href="/wiki/Bariatric_surgery" title="Bariatric surgery">bariatric surgery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Col2014_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Col2014-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGuidelines2013_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-USGuidelines2013-26">[25]</a></sup> The types of procedures include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laparoscopic_adjustable_gastric_banding" title="Laparoscopic adjustable gastric banding">laparoscopic adjustable gastric banding</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roux-en-Y_gastric_bypass" title="Roux-en-Y gastric bypass">Roux-en-Y gastric bypass</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertical-sleeve_gastrectomy" title="Vertical-sleeve gastrectomy">vertical-sleeve gastrectomy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biliopancreatic_diversion" title="Biliopancreatic diversion">biliopancreatic diversion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM_2017_207-2"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM_2017-207">[206]</a></sup> Surgery for severe obesity is associated with long-term weight loss, improvement in obesity-related conditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang2013_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chang2013-213">[212]</a></sup> and decreased overall mortality, however, improved metabolic health results from the weight loss, not the surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup> One study found a weight loss of between 14% and 25% (depending on the type of procedure performed) at 10 years, and a 29% reduction in all cause mortality when compared to standard weight loss measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup> Complications occur in about 17% of cases and reoperation is needed in 7% of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang2013_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chang2013-213">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology_of_obesity" title="Epidemiology of obesity">Epidemiology of obesity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Obesity_rate.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Obesity_rate.png/220px-Obesity_rate.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Obesity_rate.png/330px-Obesity_rate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Obesity_rate.png/440px-Obesity_rate.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Obesity_rate.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of adults that are obese (2016)</div></div></div>
<div class="mw-graph mw-graph-always mw-graph-nofallback" data-graph-id="b09eead4a05bcf37aeb70be074a48edb051f1000" style="min-width:300px;min-height:265px"></div>
<p><small>See or edit <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Data:Obesity_Males.tab" title="c:Data:Obesity Males.tab">source data</a>. </small>
</p><p><br/>
In earlier historical periods obesity was rare, and achievable only by a small elite, although already recognised as a problem for health. But as prosperity increased in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern_period" title="Early Modern period">Early Modern period</a>, it affected increasingly larger groups of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haslam2007_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haslam2007-216">[215]</a></sup> Prior to the 1970s, obesity was a relatively rare condition even in the wealthiest of nations, and when it did exist it tended to occur among the wealthy. Then, a confluence of events started to change the human condition. The average BMI of populations in first-world countries started to increase and, consequently, there was a rapid increase in the proportion of people overweight and obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1997, the WHO formally recognized obesity as a global epidemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caballero_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-113">[112]</a></sup> As of 2008, the WHO estimates that at least 500 million adults (greater than 10%) are obese, with higher rates among women than men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2009a_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009a-218">[217]</a></sup> The global prevalence of obesity more than doubled between 1980 and 2014. In 2014, more than 600 million adults were obese, equal to about 13 percent of the world's adult population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup> The percentage of adults affected in the United States as of 2015–2016 is about 39.6% overall (37.9% of males and 41.1% of females).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rate of obesity also increases with age at least up to 50 or 60 years old<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup> and severe obesity in the United States, Australia, and Canada is increasing faster than the overall rate of obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morbid2007_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-morbid2007-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24351678_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24351678-222">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tjepkema2005_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tjepkema2005-223">[222]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> has projected an increase in obesity rates until at least 2030, especially in the United States, Mexico and England with rates reaching 47%, 39% and 35%, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p>Once considered a problem only of high-income countries, obesity rates are rising worldwide and affecting both the developed and developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuroG2008_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-EuroG2008-225">[224]</a></sup> These increases have been felt most dramatically in urban settings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2009a_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009a-218">[217]</a></sup> The only remaining region of the world where obesity is not common is <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-30"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p><i>Obesity</i> is from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>obesitas</i>, which means "stout, fat, or plump". <i>Ēsus</i> is the past participle of <i>edere</i> (to eat), with <i>ob</i> (over) added to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymol_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymol-226">[225]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="The Oxford English Dictionary">The Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> documents its first usage in 1611 by <a href="/wiki/Randle_Cotgrave" title="Randle Cotgrave">Randle Cotgrave</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[226]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_attitudes">Historical attitudes</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Mellin_(attributed)_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="A very obese gentleman with a prominent double chin and mustache dressed in black with a sword at his left side." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6837" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="376" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Charles_Mellin_%28attributed%29_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Mellin_(attributed)_-_Portrait_of_a_Gentleman_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> obesity was often seen as a sign of wealth, and was relatively common among the elite: <i>The Tuscan General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alessandro_del_Borro" title="Alessandro del Borro">Alessandro del Borro</a></i>, attributed to Charles Mellin, 1645<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zach2003_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zach2003-228">[227]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg"><img alt="A carved stone miniature figurine depicted an obese female." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2684" data-file-width="1428" decoding="async" height="414" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg/220px-Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg/330px-Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg/440px-Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_von_Willendorf_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Willendorf" title="Venus of Willendorf">Venus of Willendorf</a></i> created 24,000–22,000 BC</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_medicine" title="Ancient Greek medicine">Ancient Greek medicine</a> recognizes obesity as a medical disorder, and records that the Ancient Egyptians saw it in the same way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haslam2007_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haslam2007-216">[215]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> wrote that "Corpulence is not only a disease itself, but the harbinger of others".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-31"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup> The Indian surgeon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sushruta" title="Sushruta">Sushruta</a> (6th century BCE) related obesity to diabetes and heart disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dwivedi&amp;Dwivedi07_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi&amp;Dwivedi07-229">[228]</a></sup> He recommended physical work to help cure it and its side effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dwivedi&amp;Dwivedi07_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi&amp;Dwivedi07-229">[228]</a></sup> For most of human history mankind struggled with food scarcity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[229]</a></sup> Obesity has thus historically been viewed as a sign of wealth and prosperity. It was common among high officials in Europe in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zach2003_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zach2003-228">[227]</a></sup> as well as in Ancient East Asian civilizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[230]</a></sup> In the 17th century, English medical author <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Venner" title="Tobias Venner">Tobias Venner</a> is credited with being one of the first to refer to the term as a societal disease in a published English language book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haslam2007_216-2"><a href="#cite_note-Haslam2007-216">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> it was realized that the military and economic might of nations were dependent on both the body size and strength of their soldiers and workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caballero_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-113">[112]</a></sup> Increasing the average body mass index from what is now considered underweight to what is now the normal range played a significant role in the development of industrialized societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caballero_113-3"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-113">[112]</a></sup> Height and weight thus both increased through the 19th century in the developed world. During the 20th century, as populations reached their genetic potential for height, weight began increasing much more than height, resulting in obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caballero_113-4"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-113">[112]</a></sup> In the 1950s increasing wealth in the developed world decreased child mortality, but as body weight increased heart and kidney disease became more common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caballero_113-5"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[232]</a></sup>
During this time period, insurance companies realized the connection between weight and life expectancy and increased premiums for the obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-32"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many cultures throughout history have viewed obesity as the result of a character flaw. The <i>obesus</i> or fat character in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_comedy" title="Ancient Greek comedy">Ancient Greek comedy</a> was a glutton and figure of mockery. During Christian times the food was viewed as a gateway to the sins of <a href="/wiki/Sloth_(deadly_sin)" title="Sloth (deadly sin)">sloth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lust" title="Lust">lust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-25">[24]</a></sup> In modern Western culture, excess weight is often regarded as unattractive, and obesity is commonly associated with various negative stereotypes. People of all ages can face social stigmatization, and may be targeted by bullies or shunned by their peers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bias2001_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bias2001-234">[233]</a></sup>
</p><p>Public perceptions in Western society regarding healthy body weight differ from those regarding the weight that is considered ideal  – and both have changed since the beginning of the 20th century. The weight that is viewed as an ideal has become lower since the 1920s. This is illustrated by the fact that the average height of Miss America pageant winners increased by 2% from 1922 to 1999, while their average weight decreased by 12%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[234]</a></sup> On the other hand, people's views concerning healthy weight have changed in the opposite direction. In Britain, the weight at which people considered themselves to be overweight was significantly higher in 2007 than in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John2008_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-John2008-236">[235]</a></sup> These changes are believed to be due to increasing rates of adiposity leading to increased acceptance of extra body fat as being normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John2008_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-John2008-236">[235]</a></sup>
</p><p>Obesity is still seen as a sign of wealth and well-being in many parts of Africa. This has become particularly common since the <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> epidemic began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaslamJames_2-33"><a href="#cite_note-HaslamJames-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_arts">The arts</span></h3>
<p>The first sculptural representations of the human body 20,000–35,000 years ago depict obese females. Some attribute the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venus_figurines" title="Venus figurines">Venus figurines</a> to the tendency to emphasize fertility while others feel they represent "fatness" in the people of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-25">[24]</a></sup> Corpulence is, however, absent in both Greek and Roman art, probably in keeping with their ideals regarding moderation. This continued through much of Christian European history, with only those of low socioeconomic status being depicted as obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> some of the upper class began flaunting their large size, as can be seen in portraits of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_dal_Borro" title="Alessandro dal Borro">Alessandro dal Borro</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-25">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Rubens</a> (1577–1640) regularly depicted heavyset women in his pictures, from which derives the term <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens#Work" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Rubenesque</a>. These women, however, still maintained the "hourglass" shape with its relationship to fertility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fumento_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fumento-237">[236]</a></sup> During the 19th century, views on obesity changed in the Western world. After centuries of obesity being synonymous with wealth and social status, slimness began to be seen as the desirable standard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhouse_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse-25">[24]</a></sup> In his 1819 print, <i>The Belle Alliance, or the Female Reformers of Blackburn!!!,</i> artist <a href="/wiki/George_Cruikshank" title="George Cruikshank">George Cruikshank</a> criticised the work of female reformers in <a href="/wiki/Blackburn" title="Blackburn">Blackburn</a> and used fatness as a means to portray them as unfeminine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[237]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact">Economic impact</span></h3>
<p>In addition to its health impacts, obesity leads to many problems including disadvantages in employment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puhl_R._p.29_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Puhl_R._p.29-239">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[239]</a></sup> and increased business costs. These effects are felt by all levels of society from individuals, to corporations, to governments.
</p><p>In 2005, the medical costs attributable to obesity in the US were an estimated $190.2 billion or 20.6% of all medical expenditures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medical-costs_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-medical-costs-241">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[242]</a></sup> while the cost of obesity in Canada was estimated at CA$2 billion in 1997 (2.4% of total health costs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CADG2006_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-99">[98]</a></sup> The total annual direct cost of overweight and obesity in Australia in 2005 was A$21 billion. Overweight and obese Australians also received A$35.6 billion in government subsidies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJA2009_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-MJA2009-244">[243]</a></sup> The estimate range for annual expenditures on diet products is $40 billion to $100 billion in the US alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[244]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">The Lancet</a> Commission on Obesity in 2019 called for a global treaty—modelled on the <a href="/wiki/WHO_Framework_Convention_on_Tobacco_Control" title="WHO Framework Convention on Tobacco Control">WHO Framework Convention on Tobacco Control</a>—committing countries to address obesity and undernutrition, explicitly excluding the food industry from policy development. They estimate the global cost of obesity  $2 trillion a year, about or 2.8% of world GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>Obesity prevention programs have been found to reduce the cost of treating obesity-related disease. However, the longer people live, the more medical costs they incur. Researchers, therefore, conclude that reducing obesity may improve the public's health, but it is unlikely to reduce overall health spending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[246]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wide_Chair.jpg"><img alt="An extra wide chair beside a number of normal sized chairs." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="457" data-file-width="543" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Wide_Chair.jpg/290px-Wide_Chair.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Wide_Chair.jpg/435px-Wide_Chair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Wide_Chair.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wide_Chair.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Services accommodate obese people with specialized equipment such as much wider chairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[247]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Obesity can lead to social stigmatization and disadvantages in employment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puhl_R._p.29_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-Puhl_R._p.29-239">[238]</a></sup> When compared to their normal weight counterparts, obese workers on average have higher rates of absenteeism from work and take more disability leave, thus increasing costs for employers and decreasing productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[248]</a></sup> A study examining Duke University employees found that people with a BMI over 40 kg/m<sup>2</sup> filed twice as many <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_compensation" title="Workers' compensation">workers' compensation</a> claims as those whose BMI was 18.5–24.9 kg/m<sup>2</sup>. They also had more than 12 times as many lost work days. The most common injuries in this group were due to falls and lifting, thus affecting the lower extremities, wrists or hands, and backs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[249]</a></sup> The Alabama State Employees' Insurance Board approved a controversial plan to charge obese workers $25 a month for health insurance that would otherwise be free unless they take steps to lose weight and improve their health. These measures started in January 2010 and apply to those state workers whose BMI exceeds 35 kg/m<sup>2</sup> and who fail to make improvements in their health after one year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[250]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some research shows that obese people are less likely to be hired for a job and are less likely to be promoted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bias2001_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bias2001-234">[233]</a></sup> Obese people are also paid less than their non-obese counterparts for an equivalent job; obese women on average make 6% less and obese men make 3% less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[251]</a></sup>
</p><p>Specific industries, such as the airline, healthcare and food industries, have special concerns. Due to rising rates of obesity, airlines face higher fuel costs and pressures to increase seating width.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[252]</a></sup> In 2000, the extra weight of obese passengers cost airlines US$275 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[253]</a></sup> The healthcare industry has had to invest in special facilities for handling severely obese patients, including special lifting equipment and <a href="/wiki/Bariatric_ambulance" title="Bariatric ambulance">bariatric ambulances</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[254]</a></sup> Costs for restaurants are increased by litigation accusing them of causing obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Govtrack_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Govtrack-256">[255]</a></sup> In 2005 the US Congress discussed legislation to prevent civil lawsuits against the food industry in relation to obesity; however, it did not become law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Govtrack_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-Govtrack-256">[255]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a>'s 2013 classification of obesity as a chronic disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes20130618_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes20130618-27">[26]</a></sup> it is thought that health insurance companies will more likely pay for obesity treatment, counseling and surgery, and the cost of research and development of fat treatment pills or gene therapy treatments should be more affordable if insurers help to subsidize their cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WashPost20130620_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-WashPost20130620-257">[256]</a></sup> The AMA classification is not legally binding, however, so health insurers still have the right to reject coverage for a treatment or procedure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WashPost20130620_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-WashPost20130620-257">[256]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, The European Court of Justice ruled that morbid obesity is a disability. The Court said that if an employee's obesity prevents them from "full and effective participation of that person in professional life on an equal basis with other workers", then it shall be considered a disability and that firing someone on such grounds is discriminatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[257]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_acceptance">Size acceptance</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fat_acceptance_movement" title="Fat acceptance movement">Fat acceptance movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_stigma_of_obesity" title="Social stigma of obesity">Social stigma of obesity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Health_at_Every_Size" title="Health at Every Size">Health at Every Size</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fat_fetishism" title="Fat fetishism">Fat fetishism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1117" data-file-width="694" decoding="async" height="354" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg/220px-PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg/330px-PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg/440px-PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PresidentTaftTelephoneCrop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_President" title="United States President">United States President</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> was often ridiculed for being overweight.</div></div></div>
<p>The principal goal of the fat acceptance movement is to decrease discrimination against people who are overweight and obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[259]</a></sup> However, some in the movement are also attempting to challenge the established relationship between obesity and negative health outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pulver2007_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pulver2007-261">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of organizations exist that promote the acceptance of obesity. They have increased in prominence in the latter half of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[261]</a></sup> The US-based <a href="/wiki/National_Association_to_Advance_Fat_Acceptance" title="National Association to Advance Fat Acceptance">National Association to Advance Fat Acceptance</a> (NAAFA) was formed in 1969 and describes itself as a civil rights organization dedicated to ending size discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[262]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Size_Acceptance_Association" title="International Size Acceptance Association">International Size Acceptance Association</a> (ISAA) is a <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organization</a> (NGO) which was founded in 1997. It has more of a global orientation and describes its mission as promoting size acceptance and helping to end weight-based discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[263]</a></sup> These groups often argue for the recognition of obesity as a disability under the US <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Americans_With_Disabilities_Act" title="Americans With Disabilities Act">Americans With Disabilities Act</a> (ADA). The American legal system, however, has decided that the potential public health costs exceed the benefits of extending this anti-discrimination law to cover obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pulver2007_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pulver2007-261">[260]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry_influence_on_research">Industry influence on research</span></h3>
<p>In 2015, the <i>New York Times</i> published an article on the <a href="/wiki/Global_Energy_Balance_Network" title="Global Energy Balance Network">Global Energy Balance Network</a>, a nonprofit founded in 2014 that advocated for people to focus on increasing exercise rather than reducing calorie intake to avoid obesity and to be healthy. The organization was founded with at least $1.5M in funding from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Company" title="Coca-Cola Company">Coca-Cola Company</a>, and the company has provided $4M in research funding to the two founding scientists Gregory A. Hand and <a href="/wiki/Steven_Blair" title="Steven Blair">Steven N. Blair</a> since 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NesleJAMA_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-NesleJAMA-266">[265]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_obesity">Childhood obesity</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Childhood_obesity" title="Childhood obesity">Childhood obesity</a></div>
<p>The healthy BMI range varies with the age and sex of the child. Obesity in children and adolescents is defined as a BMI greater than the 95th <a href="/wiki/Percentile" title="Percentile">percentile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc.gov_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-cdc.gov-36">[35]</a></sup> The reference data that these percentiles are based on is from 1963 to 1994 and thus has not been affected by the recent increases in rates of obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flegal_KM,_Ogden_CL,_Wei_R,_Kuczmarski_RL,_Johnson_CL_2001_1086–93_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flegal_KM,_Ogden_CL,_Wei_R,_Kuczmarski_RL,_Johnson_CL_2001_1086–93-37">[36]</a></sup> Childhood obesity has reached epidemic proportions in the 21st century, with rising rates in both the developed and the developing world. Rates of obesity in Canadian boys have increased from 11% in the 1980s to over 30% in the 1990s, while during this same time period rates increased from 4 to 14% in Brazilian children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flynn2006_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-flynn2006-267">[266]</a></sup> In the UK, there were 60% more obese children in 2005 compared to 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[267]</a></sup> In the US, the percentage of overweight and obese children increased to 16% in 2008, a 300% increase over the prior 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[268]</a></sup>
</p><p>As with obesity in adults, many factors contribute to the rising rates of childhood obesity. Changing diet and decreasing physical activity are believed to be the two most important causes for the recent increase in the incidence of child obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[269]</a></sup> Antibiotics in the first 6 months of life have been associated with excess weight at age seven to twelve years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cox2015_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2015-165">[164]</a></sup> Because childhood obesity often persists into adulthood and is associated with numerous chronic illnesses, children who are obese are often tested for <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperlipidemia" title="Hyperlipidemia">hyperlipidemia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_liver_disease" title="Fatty liver disease">fatty liver disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CADG2006_99-4"><a href="#cite_note-CADG2006-99">[98]</a></sup> Treatments used in children are primarily lifestyle interventions and behavioral techniques, although efforts to increase activity in children have had little success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23044984_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23044984-271">[270]</a></sup> In the United States, medications are not FDA approved for use in this age group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flynn2006_267-1"><a href="#cite_note-flynn2006-267">[266]</a></sup> Multi-component behaviour change interventions that include changes to dietary and physical activity may reduce BMI in the short term in children aged 6 to 11 years, although the benefits are small and quality of evidence is low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[271]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Obesity_in_pets" title="Obesity in pets">Obesity in pets</a></div>
<p>Obesity in pets is common in many countries. In the United States, 23–41% of dogs are overweight, and about 5.1% are obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lund2006_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lund2006-273">[272]</a></sup> The rate of obesity in cats was slightly higher at 6.4%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lund2006_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lund2006-273">[272]</a></sup> In Australia the rate of obesity among dogs in a veterinary setting has been found to be 7.6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[273]</a></sup> The risk of obesity in dogs is related to whether or not their owners are obese; however, there is no similar correlation between cats and their owners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[274]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<p><b>Informational notes</b>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-BogusAllometry-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-BogusAllometry_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Professor <a href="/wiki/Nick_Trefethen" title="Nick Trefethen">Nick Trefethen</a> of the University of Oxford recommends an alternative formula which replaces the square of height with a power of 5/2 (along with appropriate recentering of other coefficients), as this is known to scale more accurately for short and tall individuals, an observation dating all the way to the Belgian scientist <a href="/wiki/Adolphe_Quetelet" title="Adolphe Quetelet">Adolphe Quetelet</a> who first devised the <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a>, from his own writings in 1842.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trefethen2013_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trefethen2013-12">[12]</a></sup> The power of two was adopted instead for arithmetic convenience in an era that predated electronic calculators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trefethen2013_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trefethen2013-12">[12]</a></sup> Additionally, European male populations have increased in average height by 10–15 cm (4–6 inches) since the middle of the 19th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roser2013_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roser2013-13">[13]</a></sup> increasing the fraction of the tall male population where the commonly accepted modern BMI distorts human <a href="/wiki/Allometry" title="Allometry">allometry</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
